Title: Subway Etiquette
Post by: mcdirk on June 13, 2006, 11:21:04 AM
Am I the only one noticing this, or is lack of common courtesy on the subway trains increasing?  I keep seeing folks taking up two seats with their legs spread so far apart it looks painful, even using their knees to prod the people trying to sit next to them.  I saw an elderly lady standing as no one with a seat would get up despite the fact that it was the after-work rush last week.  I thought I'd seen it all until I saw a guy spit on the seat next to him to guarantee that it would stay empty.  That was just vile.
Maybe it's my Southern upbringing, but shouldn't folks be standing if they see someone elderly, with a disability, a baby stroller, packages or a woman who is pregnant?  I've been noticing people stopping at the top of the stairs as well - making everyone behind them come to a complete stop.  I've seen folks stop at the bottom of stairs when they realize it's not their train making everyone behind them, whose train it was, miss the train.  Is is just the Summer or are the subways becoming less civilized?

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: lanseaux on June 13, 2006, 11:34:17 AM
Quote
I saw 2 men sitting on the subway with an empty seat between them. One of them had his legs spread out and the other one had his bag in the seat. A girl was looking for a seat - they ignored her.

In this situation, I have been known to say "Excuse me," relatively loudly, and sit down.  If someone has a bag on a seat, I say, "Excuse me," and sit down when they pick it up.  On the older trains where the seats aren't defined, on average, six people can fit on a bench (I've seen 7, but that's very squished).  During rush hour, if there are five people on the bench, I make my way to the biggest gap in the seat and say relatively loudly, "Excuse me," and sit down once people move over.  If they look uncertain I add, "so I can sit down."

It works.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: merm on June 13, 2006, 01:55:12 PM
A lot of times I see this kind of thing happen and I just sit down. I figure "hey, if this guy/girl wants to take up two seats then I guess s/he doesn't mind me sitting on their lap" and I just sit right down. It's no problem, I've never gotten any trouble for doing it. I don't think people are as rude as they are being made out to be here.

This reminds me of when I lived next to Trade Fair on Steinway Street. The space between the aisles are so tight there that two people can only barely fit through. You can't help but bump into people as you weave down the row. Every once and a while somebody get's all fired up with all the "bumping" and waiting in the traffic jam and you see them standing there brooding in anger that people are so "rude" to bump into them etc. One lady in particular I remember went down every aisle of the store yelling "EXCUSE ME, EXCUSE ME" and then I heard her say "NEW YORKERS ARE SO RUDE, DOESN'T ANYONE SAY EXCUSE ME ANY MORE?!". "No," I said back, "in New York you are automatically excused". In other words, if we all went around saying "excuse me" in the grocery store and on the subway for every little bump or mistake in etiquette we'd be saying it incessantly all day long. I only say "excuse me" if I accidently run into someone or something, but not for little bumps or traffic jams along the way.

My point here is that I think the rule is the same on the subway; when some people sit down (and don't tell me that this is a male-dominated problem) they may just by habbit put their bags on the seat beside them, or spread their legs, or lean over into the next seat, or sit with their legs up on the next chair - but that doesn't automatically mean they are being rude. If you want to sit down, just sit down - chances are the people will scoot over (they do for me, and I'm not exactly small).

My motto is that in New York you're automatically excused (unless you really ram into someone or something). So I just make room for people without them saying "excuse me", but also expect others to make room for me without my having to say anything either. I just sit down and it's never been a problem. Honestly though sometimes I forget and I sit there with my legs crossed or something (which blocks the next seat) and just forget. But when someone sits down it's no problem, I just make room.

I'm not making an excuse for people who spit on the seat or whatever, but some of these stories are just the result of some of you not realizing that it's okay to just sit down I think.

I do find it mildly distrubing that it's expected that the males in the car will give up their seat if an elderly person, or if a woman comes into the car. That's both sexist against men and patronizing to women and elderly. I'm against discrimination. If someone wants to give up their seat that's an individual thing and I personally do it on a case by case basis, but any "rules" that say that people "should" act that way are just plain unfair to all involved. I had an old guy yell at me one time for singling him out and offering him my seat. He was right, what exactly am I saying by doing that? He told me if he needs my seat he'd ask for it, but didn't appreciate my making assumptions about him.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: photomill on June 13, 2006, 03:59:43 PM
Male or female, bags on seats or spread legs - people SHOULD know better than to do this.  It's a bigger issue - not simply rudeness...rather entitlement.  They are entitled to have more space than you because they got there first.  They are entitled to lean on the pole, squashing your hand, because you didn't think to lean on the pole instead.

It is rudeness.  People are not entitled.  The little bumps count. 

People do try to a certain extent - we try to act like gas molecules - allowing for the maximum distance between oneself and the next molecule.  But some people simple could care less that their actions/lack of action has an effect on those around them.

Remember the herd mentality too - no one, and I mean no one, likes the unstable isotope that upsets the herd by acting out or being the lone wolf.  It draws attention and causes tension in the other animals in the zoo that is New York City.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette

What is the proper etiquette?  If someone gives you a nasty look and seems to want to hold two seats, while only using one, should you not sit down?  I just wanted to read and not get jostled by the train. 

I have always thought one should try and accommodate others on the train.  I learned recently that this consequences.  I slid over to the edge to make room and a woman sat next to me on the bench.  It was tight, but it was the right thing to do.  When the person on the other side moved though, she stayed right up, almost in my lap.  I didn't know whether I should say something - "Um, you have several inches on the other side, why not slide over..." or just ignore it.  I was surprised that she opted to stay smushed right up against me and not have more space.  Thankfully my stop came soon and I left the train.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette

Most subway-car panhandlers have a story that they can tell in about 2 or 3 minutes - enough time to tell the story, make the rounds through the car to get cash from anyone who gives it, and then get off the train almost immediately after.

The story usually runs along the lines of "I lost my job, my husband/wife/other died, I have nowhere to sleep tonight/nothing to eat/etc., please help."

The guy I mentioned is unusual because his story went for 3 stops, which is not a best practice at all.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette

I usually give some change to the performers if they are good, and even if they aren't too talented but they have enthusiasm, I'll still contribute. My latest favorite encounter with a train performer: Kinda early one Sunday morning we were going downtown I think to walk across the Brooklyn Bridge but anyway the trains weren't crowded. We were sitting across from the door and at Queensborough this dark-haired, kindy messy but cute girl gets in the car, looks around feverishly and jumps back to the platform as the other train pulls in and is clearly torn between the two, then barely gets back in our car before the doors close. She stands at the pole right in front of us and I was looking at her, she was about 20, very cherubic-like, and I thought "I hope she's not lost". Just as the train pulled out of the station she opened her mouth and out came the most beautiful operatic melody, instantly every eye in the car was upon her. She sang almost the whole way through the tunnel and, man,what a powerful voice, you could hear her perfectly over the roar of the train. The car erupted in applause - it was fantastic! She made out pretty well, everyone in the car was giving bills including us,a (very rare) buck each. At 59th she thanked us and took a bow before getting out. I'm smiling just thinking about it. I love New York City.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: mcdirk on October 05, 2006, 03:22:16 PM
I had a very unusual experience this morning.  I got on at Broadway, as usual, and at 36th Street a guy who was probably 6'6" got on and I moved over a bit so he had room (we were in the back corner near the conductor booth, standing as there were no seats).  By Queensboro Plaza, he had moved over to stand next to the bench and I had a big more room and slid over to the other side of the Conductors door.  At QBP, a guy who must have been 6'9" got on, and did not move over to the center of the car, but rode stooped over in the lower part of the car.  I don't think he could raise his head even if he wanted to.  Unfortunately, he had a big backpack and I was right about eye level with it and had no place to which I could escape.  I spent the ride under the East River and until 57th Street when the car emptied out a bit with it hitting me repeatedly, though not as badly as it could.  It was just a weird and strange situation.  I don't think there was any place for him to put the bag, and the train was packed, but I don't get why he didn't move to where he could stand up.  I'd never seen anything like it and to be trapped in the low level part of the car with two guys who could barely stand up there was just odd.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: megc on December 20, 2006, 03:55:55 PM
I just read this, from AMNY's Subway Tracker blog (http://weblogs.amny.com/news/local/tracker/blog/2006/12/subway_etiquette.html).  As one who currently rides one of the most unpleasant trains during commute hours (the 7, and thank goodness that'll be over soon), it resonated well with me:

Quote
Im the self-proclaimed King of Subway Etiquette. Heres my four biggest issues with subway riders. These apply year-round, but are especially true during the holiday season.

Let people off first, then you can get on. (Goes for elevators as well. What kind of idiot gets on the subway before anyone has had a chance to get off? Yet it happens all the time. When it happens to me, I push straight through the person trying to get on first until he or she gives up and backs away. The entire time Im looking at the offender and saying semi-loudly Let em off first. Let em off first. Let em off first. Offenders usually think that I am either 1.) really tough; or 2.) really crazy. (Truth: a little bit of both.)

Bags, backpacks, work bags, big purses, etc. do NOT go on your shoulder, but on the ground between your feet on a crowded subway car. Bags on your shoulder take up a tremendous amount of space, especially considering that people are generally bigger (and fatter, like me) on top, rather than the bottom. And every time you turn with a bag or backpack slung on your shoulder, youre hitting someone. Thats right, Im talking to you. YOU ARE BANGING INTO ME WITH YOUR BAG. STOP IT. NOW.

Putting your bag on the seat next to you. Or even THINKING about putting your bag on the seat next to you. Do you really think the subway car isnt going to get crowded? And when it does and your bag is on the seat, are you saying that YOUR BAGs comfort is more important than another HUMAN BEINGS comfort? And the look you get when you dare sit down where someones bag is sitting! Its like the offender is saying cant you see that this is my bags seat? Where do expect my bag to go? On my lap? Oh no. And my bag will not stand while you sit. Swear to God, I came about two inches away from sitting on someones bag before they moved it. After I sat down, I just stared at them the entire time. Cuz thats good manners.

For Gods sake, hold onto something. A rail. A strap. An armrest at the end of the seating section. You may think you have balance better than Tony Hawk and Bode Miller combined, but you dont. Youre a 40-year-old, out-of-shape, one pack a day fogey and you should be holding on for dear life. Youre lucky youre standing with the help of a railing, let alone without one.

Thats item. The gospel according to me. Id write more, but its time for my medication.

Regards,
Bob Zeitlinger
Managing Director

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: mcdirk on June 22, 2007, 08:08:35 AM
Folks with bags really need to take them off on the train.  I had a woman who seemed very nice, but incredibly oblivious move over to sit between me and her friend on the W last night.  She was, however, wearing a big bulky backpack - which immediately was on me and in my lap.  After a minute or two of annoyance and shock, I moved to her old seat on the other side of the train - at which point she said, "Sorry."  That's nice, but if you're wearing a big bag and you feel it hitting something, and the only thing it can be hitting is the person sitting behind you, who you don't know, why not just take the bag off and put it on the floor?  I just don't get what folks are thinking.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: felisfemina on June 22, 2007, 10:48:29 AM
What I'm about to say will, most likely, not win me any fans but, well, too bad...

With the exception of very pregnant women or very frail looking elderly people, I rarely give up my seat.  I spend the better part of my day on my feet and when I get on the subway all I want to do is sit down.  My legs and feet ache and I'm usually carrying a backpack filled with large science books.  I'm not being rude, if someone gets on the train who looks like they won't make it one stop without falling over I'll give up my seat, but just because I'm fairly young and healthy doesn't mean that I don't need to get off my feet.  My philosophy is, this is a city of walkers.  If you managed to get yourself to the subway, and you're going to manage to get yourself wherever you're going when you get off, then you'll be just fine if, perchance there is no seat for you.

Maybe not the most popular opinion, but that's the truth.
Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: mcdirk on June 22, 2007, 11:02:08 AM
I always give up my seat for the elderly, pregnant women and folks with baby carriages, or big bulky packages, but I don't think folks should have to give up a seat just because folks are standing, but I do think people should try and share the bench.  These men (it's almost, though not always) who sit with their legs spread so wide that they take up two to three seats just make steam come out my ears.  It looks so uncomfortable - is it just territorial activity?  I always try to make space - it just seems cruel to leave someone standing if there is room on the bench.  What's with folks using newspapers to hold an extra seat?  That's just being a jerk, imo.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: essen on June 23, 2007, 02:34:48 AM
It's presumptuous to think that just because you are a certain way means you deserve someone else's seat. What if that person has had a long day on their feet, or if they have bad joints, or if they're feeling dizzy or sick? You can't tell these things by looking at them. No one should assume that just because someone's young means they're healthy or feeling great at any given time.

Generally what I like doing is, if I'm sitting between two empty seats and a couple people get on that are friends (or mother and child or something) I'll move over so they can sit next to each other. That way we're all happy. But I don't think anyone should "expect" a seat just because they appear one way and someone else appears another way.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: schmennifer on June 23, 2007, 03:56:51 PM
Quote from: Chris on June 23, 2007, 09:15:42 AM
And I agree about the packages on seats.   I had the same thing happen the other night.   A full train and this woman is sitting there with her bags on the seat.   I asked to her to move it and sat down and it wasn't an issue for her, but when the train started to fill up she should have moved her package/bags to her lap.

I'm pretty sure people can get fined for leaving their bags and packages on seats. According to the rules of conduct (http://mta.info/nyct/rules/), it's a violation to "place one's foot on the seat of a subway, bus, or platform bench; occupy more than one seat or place bags on an empty seat when doing so would interfere with transit operations or the comfort of other customers." I know someone who got fined $60 for putting her foot on a seat when her car was all but empty after 1 a.m., so I imagine that one could be ticketed for any of the other stuff.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: eblack on June 23, 2007, 08:58:14 PM
Quote from: Alexa on June 23, 2007, 06:00:37 PM
Uh...no...what Essen interpreted is right on the mark from my perspective. Ofcourse it is all subjective...this isn't personal, and at least from my comments, your interpretation is off.

Sorry, Alexa, I guess I interpreted your comment, "I will NOT give up my seat to someone that has a baby carriage" incorrectly.

I think we can all agree (I hope) that it really comes down to just plain being considerate.  I try to be as considerate as possible when I have the stroller on the subway.  I make every attempt possible to get a seat that's out of the way and pull the stroller in to me as closely as possible. 

If someone has their stroller blocking the doorway and have the opportunity to move out of the way, they should.  If they don't, then they suck, fair enough.  But if there's no room for them to move on a crowded subway, they don't have a choice.  As another passenger, at that point, I would give them my seat, not so much so that they can sit, but so they can get out of everyone else's way.  I've had people shove past me to get the two person seats at the ends of the N cars when there are plenty of other seats and that's the best place to get the stroller out of the way.  It takes all kinds, I guess...

Speaking of subway etiquette, today im riding the train (N Train of course!) and some girl is continously shoving me during the trip. As far as giving up seats, it really depends on a lot of factors. Its all about observation - if someone is having any type of issue causing difficulty standing (older person thats not in good condition, disabled person, visibly/obviously pregnant woman (use good judgement to avoid offending someone that is just chunky), someone injured, someone carrying a child or a pet)  then I offer my seat. Im on the fence with strollers. If Im sitting in an area where a stroller would comfortably fit then I would, but if im sitting near a poll or somewhere that may obstruct people from moving in or out the train then I dont. I usually stand anyway bc I have this whole thing about subway seats (thats a whole 'nother issue).

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: jumpattack on July 01, 2007, 05:30:49 PM
Quote from: mcw on July 01, 2007, 05:25:38 PM
I've experienced incidents twice so far in which I was sitting in the outside seat of the forward-facing two-seater, and was asked in a demanding way to give up my seat for someone who didn't just want to sit down in the inside seat, but who wanted *my* seat.

When you sit on the outside of the forward-facing two seater, you are effectively blocking the inside seat. It's a pain in the ass to move past the person sitting in the outer seat to get to the inner seat, so the polite thing to do, if you are sitting in the outer seat, is to slide over to the inner seat. I think those people were right to ask you to move.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: SpecialK on July 01, 2007, 09:46:51 PM
When I was recovering from gallbladder surgery a couple of years ago, I was sitting in the outside seat of the two-seater when a short woman came along and asked me to move over.  I pointed out that being nearly an Amazon, I can't really fit in there (most of my height is in my legs), happily offered to do the 90 degree thing so she could get in.  She said that no, she had to have that seat because she had bad knees.  At this point I retorted that I also have bad knees (two years of teaching special ed preschool with emergency lifting of tantrumming children on staircases can do that to you) and was also recovering from recent surgery, but she proceeded to escalate the argument until all I could do was stand weakly.  I don't like arguing.

Someone else who had overheard offered me a seat, and I was very grateful for it.

I really wish I'd pointed out to her that there are in fact seats reserved for people with disabilities, and since I had mine, she should have taken one of those, but I didn't.  I was fairly shaken by the whole encounter, honestly.

Whoever said you can't always see disability is right - I've had days where I don't mind standing, and also days when I need my seat.  That was one of them, and that woman was foul.

Oh, and she works in my industry.  At the time I didn't know who she was, but now I know exactly where she works.  You never know when that kind of thing can come back around to you.

On a better note, I was very relieved about the courtesy of N train riders when I sprained my ankle last winter.  While I was on crutches and visibly hurt, people were quick to offer seats.  Once I was over the crutches I was still having balance issues, and was lucky enough to get a seat most days, but one day the train was packed and since I 'looked' normal I decided to tough it out and hang on for dear life on the way home.  I just didn't feel right asking people to give up their seats when I was walking fairly normally and appeared perfectly healthy.

At Fifth Ave the train did the sudden brake thing and I wasn't able to hold my weight on the injured ankle, which ended with me tumbling into a couple of people (yes, it was humiliating and yes, I apologized quickly).  Someone overheard me laughing about it and explaining the state of my physical therapy to the coworker I was with, and offered me a seat.  Whoever it was, thank you! 

It wasn't one of the people I'd fallen on, either.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: NYCMacUser on July 14, 2007, 11:14:10 AM
Quote from: Alexa on July 14, 2007, 10:26:20 AM
I interpret this the same way. Does a drink with a straw constitute an open container? Enquiring minds want to know.
Yes. Which is why you see so many young people drinking beer through a straw in a paper bag on the street. If you carry an open container of any alcoholic beverage on the street, or any open container of anything on public transportation, you are subject to a fine. Open simple means not sealed, or it is considered unopened. If you can drink from the contents of the container, it is opened. Please bear in mind that they won't even let you take a sealed bottle of water on an airplane. IMHO, the hassle just isn't worth it!

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: eeeniebean on July 17, 2007, 02:25:42 PM
I'll agree that there seems to be an increase in both intrusive behavior and attitude on the subways lately... but I suspect that might be a function of my traveling during rush hour while working 9-5 during the summer. Everything is just "more" when people are in a rush.

But here's a question for you - people rarely complain about people talking to each other on the subway. They might complain about people yelling or making a scene or being idiots.. but not for talking. Why? Because everyone considers that normal. We all do that - if we're riding with a friend, we're probably going to be talking to them about something. Right? We aren't expected to sit in silence, next to each other. This isn't a funeral, it's the morning commute.

So then, why are people annoyed when people talk, -at the SAME volume as an in person conversation-, on a cell phone? It isn't necessarily the content of the conversation that bothers the overhearer, right? It isn't really the volume..(I don't believe that people are always louder when talking into a phone, just because it's a phone. Some people do- but not ALWAYS). So what is it about watching/hearing people on the phone that is intrinsically annoying to people?

Is it because:
- It's still new and we still haven't gotten used to people talking on the phone in public yet?
- The listener feels left out/excluded from the conversation because you can only eavesdrop on one side?
- It breaks the peaceful silence (in this case, the tranquility of the screeching, thumping aboveground subway with loud but incoherent announcements, occasionally screaming children and -other- people's conversations to each other in person? *grin*)
- Because some people have been rude about the usage of their cell phones, so now all cellphone use is presumed annoying?

Don't get me wrong - I'm annoyed by people on their phones sometimes too:
- if they're being too loud for their environment,
- if they're talking about topics that are annoying,
- if they aren't paying attention and therefore run into or inconvenience people...

But all those activities stand alone from cell phone use. It is possible to use a cell phone without being rude, just as it's possible to talk to your friend on the subway without being rude.

I guess I'm just trying to pose the question about -why- we're annoyed by particular things. Why is someone having a one-sided conversation an intrusion on your well-being if two people having a two-sided conversation aren't?
Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: daisy on July 17, 2007, 02:31:27 PM
Quote from: eeeniebean on July 17, 2007, 02:25:42 PM

I guess I'm just trying to pose the question about -why- we're annoyed by particular things. Why is someone having a one-sided conversation an intrusion on your well-being if two people having a two-sided conversation isn't?

I've always wondered the same thing.  I understand some people are just plain rude and loud on their phones, but some people are just plain rude and loud while talking to each other on the train.  I tend to be annoyed by both.  But I'll have a conversation with friends on the train all the time, so I can't complain.  Interesting paradox.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: Alexa on July 17, 2007, 06:47:21 PM
Quote from: eeeniebean on July 17, 2007, 02:25:42 PM


- The listener feels left out/excluded from the conversation because you can only eavesdrop on one side?

REsearch studies support this. Cognitive dissonance is established when we only hear one end of the conversation, requiring more cognitive energy to decipher the meaning, leaving us less cognitive energy to attend to our own tasks. Its very interesting. I'll find a link to the journal.
Title: Re: Subway Etiquette

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: Alexa on July 17, 2007, 07:19:39 PM
http://www.informaworld.com/smpp/content~content=a713819773~db=all

Here is a link to the abstract. Not sure if you can get the whole article, BUT even when noise volume was the same for cell phone people and face-to-face people (controlled), conversations that were 1-sided got higher "annoyance" scores. So it isn't necessarily that they actually are louder, we just get more annoyed with the one-sided conversation.

I politely told him, when all of a sudden he makes his hand into a gun and starts mock shooting me...then he turns and runs and i mean runs down the platform.....Crazy right....wait it gets better....
As the train pulls into the station I see that he is running back in my direction... I am thinking "what the hell?" I get on the train and he gets in the same cart as me but on the other side...I am seated on the opposite end next to the doors to the next car next to 5 MEN..... well he comes over to me and stands in front of me and his knuckles are all BLOODY right in my FACE, and he tells me in a very polite voice " excuse me I seemed to have cut myself, do you have any tissue" I was like " no sorry" 2 guys seated next to me move away..... Crazy man sits next to me ....... all of a sudden he completely changes and he is screaming ....you are the devil.....i will clean you I am your savior" the god damn train is express and there was no where for me to go...so I stand up and he gets behind me and starts shooting me again....when fffffaaaarrrr on the other side of the train and man finally stood up and asked it there was a problem.....crazy man ran into the next car after that.....
crazy thing is the next day I saw him again and he was looking at me crazy on the platform ( i was standing next to a MTA employee) I had to change my routine to work cause he really freaked me out....
My question is???? nobody could come to my defense... this homeless man has his bloody hands in my face  ( i am a 26 year old woman that hardly is 5feet) and not one of those men could come to my help sooner....no instead they get up and move to the other side of the car.......
But I love the train and I will continue to use it crazy people and all  :lol:

Post by: Astoria Luv on July 17, 2007, 09:59:56 PM
Quote from: megc on July 17, 2007, 07:22:14 PM
On the train this morning the woman next to me was on her cell phone having a conversation.  She talked in a normal tone of voice, and I could hardly hear her over the train noise!  I appreciated that she didn't broadcast her conversation.

On the rare occassion that I am on cell phone on the train, less now because my entire trip is below ground  :-( , I put my hand over mouth and try to talk as low as possible and keep the conversation to a minimum.

When I'm talking on the train, I usually try to sit next to the person I am talking to and keep my voice down.  I used to not realize how loud I could be until enough of my friends told me that voice really "carried," which was just a nice way to tell me to keep it down.  Now the same people tell me that they can't hear me when I talk  :lol

I have not been in the subway for years but opted to do so today due to the street closures in Manhattan.  I took the #7 back and then switched to the N.  When I got on the 7, there was one seat open which was a bit tight.  Mind you, my butt is not tiny, but I am not a large person either.  I opted to sit.  No one moved so I wound up sitting on the edge of my seat.  There was a prima donna next to me who made it clear that she was disturbed by the fact that I was sitting there.  I stared her down and gave her a dirty look.  Is there a new rule that we should not sit even if there is a space unless we fit into that space?  Let's face it, most of us are not a size six.  I had heard that these subway cars were manufactured in Japan, so the seats are small.  Should I not have sat down?
Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: mcdirk on July 19, 2007, 10:44:52 PM

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: eblack on July 19, 2007, 11:51:53 PM
It usually depends.  Some seats you can tell how many people will fit.  The older trains with the obvious seats are simple to figure out, but the N trains usually fit 6 "average" sized people.  By average, I mean people who aren't HUGE.  Sometimes I get in and look at the seat and if there are 5 people, I'll find the biggest little space and say "excuse me" and start to sit down.  Usually, it opens up.

However, if 5 of those 6 people are small, it might appear as though someone else could fit, but they really couldn't comfortably and I don't bother.

Totally depends on the situation.  I'd say the rule of thumb is, if you're not going to be more comfortable sitting than standing, don't sit.

I turned to them and reflexively yelled "jesus christ"...one of the girls said sorry, and as she got on the subway, whispered "bitch"...a friend of hers whispered "she's pregnant" to which the original pusher responded "i don't care"....so if I had cold-cocked her, would that have been wrong? It took every fiber of my being to keep myself undercontrol...(they weren't native to queens from their conversation although they got off at the ditmars stop...

I'm still upset that i contained myself.

So one time on the subway, Im sitting there in a full car, a woman comes onto the train.  Is she pregnant, is she a little overweight?  Cant tell.  Im guessing she is overweight and just know that if I offer her my seat and she is not pregnant Im going to seriously offend her in front of the whole car.  So I do nothing.  Few minutes later the woman next to me gives me a big "HRMPH!" gets up and gives the woman her seat. They start saying loudly how rude men are these days, wont even give up a seat to a pregnant woman. 

I knew I could never fully explain myself, they would never believe me.  Just sat there in shame.

I know 100% what you are talking about. My rationale for women whom you can't tell with is that I'd rather play it safe and not offer her a seat for the following reason: I believe it is a far worse offense to imply a woman is overweight than to not offer a seat up to a pregnant lady who's not that far along. Most pregnant women will just assume that it is NY and people are generally rude, but I wouldn't want to send some woman home feeling bad about herself because a guy on the subway mistakenly thought she was preggos. 
When I can tell a woman is pregnant, though, I will always offer my seat up. Same goes for elderly folks and women with babies strapped to those weird backpack slingy things. It amazes me how rude people are in that respect.
On that same note, I always help mothers carry strollers up and down subway stairs. It baffles me how so many people walk by and see a mother struggling like that. It takes like 30 seconds out of your day and it's not like you run into that situation that often.

I think I must be becoming more easily frustrated with folks.  I almost said something unkind today on my way into the office.  I get to the bottom of the stairs at the Broadway station just in time to get cut off by a woman a few years older than me, maybe early 40s.  She's clearly not dressed for work, unless it's a very casual office, and opts to go up the stairs incredibly slowly, in the middle of the stairs so you can not get past, on both flights.  I hear the train coming, I'm already late and eager not to be later, and tried hard not to get too close, but to be ready to move past should the opportunity present itself.  Alas, she made sure that me, and the other unfortunate people behind us got to take a very leisurely walk up the stairs, then had the great idea to stop at the top of the stairs and turn around to give me a nasty look, what joy!  I got around her at that point and just made the train.  What makes a person park themselves in the MIDDLE of a staircase blocking people clearly dressed for work (in a suit) and goes slowly when they hear the train moving into a station?  I almost never say something, I just think it's rude, but what in the world could this person be thinking?  If I'm going to move slowly - say I had an intense leg workout or sprained something, I would move to the side of the staircase and let folks pass - wouldn't most folks do that?

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: firelight79 on April 29, 2008, 03:51:18 PM
I'm going to change it up a bit and share 2 GOOD subway stories that happened yesterday.

On the R on the way to work yesterday I started feeling wicked dizzy and warm. Start peeling off the layers and hoping someone would get off at QP-no luck in my vicinity. Starting to feel weak in the knees and hoping I can just make it through the tunnel, guaranteed a seat at Lex. I can't. I feel like my face should be ghost white since I feel so clammy and like there's no blood up there. I cave-I ask a woman if she wouldn't mind if I had her seat since I really wasn't feeling well. No problem no attitute she got her s___ and got up to stand where I was and I proceeded to sit with my head between my knees til 23rd street. I really must have looked bad, because it didn't take much for her to get up.
Karma rewarded her with a seat at Lex in a few minutes and I didn't pass out thus being the sick passenger gumming up the works.

On the R train back home yesterday there was a middle seat that had a small puddle of water on it. No one decided to sit there until Lex when everyone and their mother comes in through the last door of the last car. One woman decides she'll just sit on the edge. The woman next to her takes out some tissue and wipes the seat dry for her. I thought that was super nice.

Thought I would share to temporarily put our faith back into humanity. Until the next idiot comes along...

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: lanseaux on June 24, 2008, 03:23:54 PM
I once pulled an all-nighter at work, and didn't manage to leave to head home until around 4pm the following day.  This was back when there was no such thing as "office casual", so I was in a suit with a briefcase.  I caught the early rush home, so the car was moderately full - although I got a seat.  Somewhere after QBP, I simply fell sound asleep from sheer fatigue.  When the train pulled into Ditmars, a woman gently shook me awake, and I found myself with my head resting on her shoulder - without another soul in the car.  When I had fallen asleep, I apparently leaned against her.  Instead of waking me or jumping up, she let me rest there, and didn't even move when other seats opened up.

Very different scenario to yours, mcdirk, and I don't recommend falling asleep on the subway, of course, but I'll never forget that woman's kindness.

I am certainly behind you regarding the elderly or disabled but I'll have to say the pregnant woman issue is a tougher one. The first and probably the toughest issue is determining whether or not the woman is actually pregnant. If she is late in her term it is usually pretty obvious, but there are definite grey areas. Title: Re: Subway Etiquette

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: casicua on July 17, 2008, 05:00:01 PM
I've said it on here before, and I'll say it again: I'd rather offend a pregnant woman by not offering her a seat then offend a non-pregnant woman by calling her pregnant. Title: Re: Subway Etiquette

I've often been offered a seat and it never occured to me that anyone might think I was pregnant. I figured they were just very gentlemanly, or I looked tired, or I was weighted down with packages. Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: enigmacat on July 18, 2008, 10:52:19 AM
Sure, it's sexist, but it's a pretty common attitude in my parent's generation (not assuming anything about the poster's age here), and I've never wanted to be one of those feminists who get pissed off when a man holds the door for me. I just smile, say thanks, and when I'm first through the door I'll hold it for whoever is behind me.

Our whole society is so interwoven with sexism, it's kind of hard for any one of us, no matter how aware we are, to be completely untainted by it. And to me this seems one of the less virulent forms. It's not as if it's a new and dangerous trend, and pretty soon if we aren't careful men will be standing up and letting women sit on the subway like mad. It's very much on its way out. This seems sweet and old-fashioned, like something my grandpa would have done, and something I would smile at even if I also see the sexist aspect of it.

As casicua says, a sexism that is not misogynistic may be a step in the right direction :)

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: casicua on July 18, 2008, 01:32:56 PM
At the risk of sounding like a 25 year old sexist dinosaur, why is it a problem to treat women differently if it's in a good way? I know that there is the underlying sexism of "oh, men think they're just bigger, stronger and better all the time" within those actions, but that isn't always the case. Yes, I treat women differently, I am generally more pleasant to women and I am more willing to help women than men. If that makes me a sexist, then I'll gladly wear that badge.
Watch an old-timey movie, and look at how guys used to treat women. I know there was all sorts of tacit and blatant sexism back then, but we have culturally evolved enough at least to recognize the bad from the good. I know it's easy getting caught up in gender equality these days, but let's not forget the good things we're letting go and overlooking in the process.
If I hold the door for you, and you're thinking in your head that I'm a sexist for it, please let me know so I can keep a look out for you and maybe you can open the door for me next time  :-)

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: JustDesserts on July 18, 2008, 04:40:29 PM
A man on the subway once asked everyone within a two-foot radius for a tissue to give me when I had a contact lens malfunction and my right eye streamed like a leaky faucet. I was too embarrassed to ask around myself, so I was extremely grateful to him. The fact that he noticed I was suffering was pretty amazing, since most folks on the subway seem to go out of their way to not notice the people around them (like I do). I don't think he did it because I'm a woman; I think he was just a plain ole nice guy. It seems a shame to be suspicious of other people's random acts of selflessness.

Same kind of deal on the airplane, on my way back to NY from CA. As soon as that seatbelt sign is turned off, everyone jumps and grabs their crap out of the overhead bins. My bag was small and not heavy, easily accessible and I was in no one's way to get it. However, the gentleman in my row asked if he could get it for me. Could be because I'm 5'4" and he was 6" plus and he figured it was just easier for him ... could be because I'm a woman, or he figured out I was depressed being back in NY, I don't know, but it warmed my heart a bit. It was just a nice thing for him to do ... I thought.

I dig chivalry. Makes me feel a little special when I'm so used to feeling very much the opposite. A door opened for me or a seat given up to me by a man because I'm a woman actually kind of makes my day. Maybe I need to lay off the Austen.

All that said, I don't think (and I believe I have stated this before) anyone should feel obligated to relinquish a seat on the subway (or open a door, or let a woman go into the building first, etc). Sure, it's nice, but the first-come-first-served adage applies to the train. In my opinion, humble though it may be.

I've considered investing in a cattle prod. 

I hate this kind of thing, and unfortunately there doesn't seem to be a polite way to deal with it.  I once asked a guy to please lean in the other direction (he was on the end), and he called me a nasty name.

I am not sure why she had to make it a racial issue.  The issue is really that she has less class and intelligence than most of the people on the train who don't want to listen to petty arguments after a hard day's work.  She could have been Inuit for all I know or care.  Miss, if you are reading this, I apologize for shushing you.  Clearly, you were entitled to make noise on the train, because, as you put it, you "paid your taxes to be up in the train" just like everyone else.  Please don't have children.

Was anyone else on the train with me?  If so, I am embarassed by my behavior.

I wasn't on that train, but if I was, I would have appreciated that someone told her to shut up!  If everyone would just be quiet and considerate of others, the subway wouldn't be so bad...

But really, does anyone react gracefully to being admonished in public by a stranger? It's something that inevitably evokes an emotional response. One may feel wronged and indignant, or feel guilty and humiliated. It's not likely one will be sanguine, and even less likely, grateful.

If indeed you were "less than polite", the person may have concluded that you are of no better class than she is. Hence, race must be the issue.  It's probably immaterial - I don't think Americans consider their class to be inferior to any other. Most individuals calibrate their sense of what's appropriate to place themselves squarely in the center of a spectrum, with uncouth asses in one direction, and priggish asses in the other.

Might be blatant, but every day I see dozens of people on the way to or on the subway train who are oblivious to their surroundings while they openly flash devices (mostly iPhones, Blackberries and iPods) that are worth hundreds of dollars.  Once upon a time, flashing even a watch on the subway was considered risky behavior.  In my mind, a lot of these people are perfect candidates for bash-and-grab theft, especially after dark.  Many times, it's been made clear to me, coming up from behind on someone using one of these things (and I can always tell, the way the arms are hunched in and raised, the nodded head, the slower/meandering gait, the obvious decrease of that cognitive "recognition of surroundings" physical alterness, list goes on ...), it would be relatively easy to clock/rob them before they knew what happened.  I'm not even criminal-minded and I see this.  (Then again, if I was a thief, I'd make myself aware of GPS cababilities on some of these things ...)

As it is, elbowed a guy in the face the other night who came up on me, after dark, in a way that suggested he was going to jump me from behind.  He was an iPod user and shocked that I would do something like that.  A short lecture on respecting personal space and a barbed apology helped ease the tension -- but I'd do the same thing again if someone approached me like that.  And I have and use an IPod every day!  One of the worst sins of city life is being oblivious of your surroundings:  no excuses.

Take classes at New York Sports Club -- various locations, they have a pretty healthy schedule of classes/instructors, most of which provide a great workout.  They don't allow sparring, which I'll sometimes do at Kingsway, which is on 28th and 5th in Manhattan.  (Can't afford both gyms, so I'll pay visitor fees at Kingsway through my instructor at NYSC.)

Realized after the fact how nutty my description sounded without details -- this incident happened on a very dark side street about 10 pm on a Monday night, no one else around, sound of a man's footsteps rapidly approaching (I'm a fast walker), expecting him to pass me on the left as there was plenty of space, but quickly realized someone was walking behind me, less than a foot away, a few beats too long.  Didn't pause to look back or wonder who it was -- just did my thing, and it turned out to be an early 20ish guy lost in iPodland.  Not sure if he was trying to be a wise-ass or spaced out, but you don't put your body that close to someone in a situation like that, unless you're up to no good, stoned, wildly unattentive or all or the above.

Well it has been very cold lately... If I were homeless, I'd prefer the subway to a freezing sidewalk. It's completely underground, thus warmer. Ride the N/W/R to 34th st and transfer to the PATH train to Newark, Hoboken or Journal Square. Egads, is it filthy. Even before you enter the PATH you'll notice the change in the tunnel down from the subway. The floor is clean until right where the subway ends and the PATH starts! Then the pee smell hits you... :P

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: sabatizer on February 22, 2009, 07:09:15 PM
A few months ago, my wife and I took a trip to Buenos Aires. At the time her pregnancy was just barely starting to show, and - no matter how crowded a subway train was - every man (in a seat) that we stood near would offer her the seat. Little stickers on the train windows revealed that offering seats to pregnant women was mandatory by law, but I believe there is a cultural pride and courtesy too around pregnant women there. Men were offended if my wife didn't take the seat they offered; quite a few times they stood and left the seat empty rather than be seen sitting beside a standing pregnant woman.

Of course now back in NYC, with my wife is entering her 9th month of pregnancy, it is rare when she is offered a seat. I think I may petition the MTA to put up some of those stickers from the B.A. Metro! ;)

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: megc on February 25, 2009, 08:21:18 PM
While I'm not pregnant, I saw subway etiquette from a different perspective today.  I sprained my ankle something awful a little over a week ago and it's so bad that my friend loaned my her spiffy cane for extra support as I hobble down the street (canes really work!).  I was on the subway going home today and on the N train not one person offered me a seat.  I knew people saw the cane, and one guy looked at me before he hurried over to a seat that had just become available.  Many people were spacing out on their ipods, text messaging, or just really into whatever it was that was on their mind. 

I'm lucky that I could still manage without the cane, but what if I was someone who couldn't?  What if I was a 40 year old with MS?  Or an elderly person that got tired easily from standing?  Or a pregnant person who suffers from horrible back aches?  I think a lot of people are simply not paying attention; so often it's crowded and uncomfortable that we get used to tuning a lot out on the subways.   So, I think a lot of people are more thoughtless rather than malicious.  This is not an excuse, but more of an observation.

I would like to see more kindness and care towards the people around us.  Is that too much to ask?

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette

That's something that I think is often overlooked in these types of discussions. Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: psychopompos on March 05, 2009, 10:11:43 PM
An interesting story that probably hasn't happened to too many of you. Last week I get on the almost empty last car of the Jamaica bound E at Roosevelt and the smell of bums hits me right in the nose - nothing unusual I say to myself and I try to take a seat that's rather far from them. Problem is that they're right in the middle of the car so sitting or standing very far is somewhat impossible. What happened next was that one of the bum takes some stinking cigarette from behind his ear and lights it up. So what are my options at this point: to spend 6 minutes in a smelly and smoky train, to do something illegal and possibly dangerous by moving to the other car or to risk getting assaulted by two smelly old guys. I decided that there were at least a couple of guys who weren't happy with them either and would've stood up for me if something happened so I took my headphones out of my ears and said with a big smile and my thick accent (completely natural): "Do you mind putting it out?" He did, I said: "Thank you" and that was it.

So what was my point? First, people do a lot of inappropriate things in NYC Transit but smoking isn't usually one of them. Second, if you want something to happen, you have to speak up. If I'd kept my mouth shut the guy could've lit a second one after the finished the first. Of course, beware of your surroundings and don't tell a group of 15 loud high-school kids to shut up. Best you're going to get away with is even more shouting.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: essen on March 11, 2009, 06:16:13 PM
Quote from: broadwaybaby on March 11, 2009, 12:37:57 PM
I just need a place to share my story b/c it's been a few days and it's still bugging me! 

I got on a new N train (read: no 'individual' seats) at 14th St and sat next to a pretty large individual in what is supposed to be a three seater.  The space between us was so small that I really didn't think anyone else would attempt to sit there, but of course, a few stops later, someone did.  This woman, probably in her 30s, squeezed in between us and smashed her heavy bag (which felt like it contained three laptops.. or you know, bricks) onto my knee.  Being that I still had a long ride home to Astoria, I asked, "Can you move your bag a little?" and was met with the response, "NO!  Why don't you take a LIMO? You know what that is? It's short for LIMOUSINE.  You ever heard of that?"  Not wanting to agitate her any further, I resigned to the fact that it would be a tight ride and said, "It was just a simple question.  Forget about it."  Any sane person would have ended it there, but instead she screamed, yes SCREAMED, "SHUT UP!!!".  I couldn't help it, I had to laugh.

She got off before me in Astoria and on her way out, swung her heavy bag at me in an attempt to physically hurt me.  She missed.  Man, I wish I had tripped her.  Is that bad? 

She probably would've attempted to murder you. You gotta assume in situations like that that the lady is crazy as soon as she sits down. You knew this before you said anything because she squeezed into a space that was too big for her along with a big, heavy bag that she hit you with. Clearly your comfort was not a concern of hers, and the fact that you spoke up was an affront to her. If you must protest in situations like that, I find it's best to do so in a way that appears oblivious.

A few days ago, I was on a rush hour R train and there was a dude leaning against the one pole I could get to. Now, there was a slight gap between one section of his back and the pole that I could grab onto, but unfortunately he was wearing a big messenger bag with the strap running down the full length of the pole. Instead of asking this guy to hold onto the pole properly, or asking him to move his bag so I could hold onto the pole without his strap pressing against my hand, I held onto the strap and the pole at the same time. This meant that if he swayed at all, he would get pulled back because I was essentially holding on to his bag. He glanced over at me a couple times when this happened, but I just stared blankly. He apparently wasn't bothered enough to move, or he realized that he was being a dick for standing like that in the first place and he didn't want to move regardless, but for me the aggravation turned into vague amusement on my way to work.

As for how this strategy would apply to your scenario.. I dunno, I probably would've found a way to disgust her without making it look like I was doing it on purpose. I liked Yaxpac's idea of the involuntary spasm. Sometimes the only way to fend off crazy people is to out-crazy them.

Post by: jennifer on March 16, 2009, 08:56:21 PM
So i was sitting on a packed train the other day in one of the seats designated for the disabled when I heard someone say "Excuse Me" and so I looked up.  There was a man who was probably in his 50s standing over the seat and when he got my attention he calmly and quietly said - "i'm disabled and I'd like to sit there." 

Oh - okay and I got up.  By looking at him - you never would have known that he was disabled - but as he tried to maneuver into the seat in such a packed area it was pretty apparent.  In the meantime the guy sitting next to me (closer to the disabled guy) continued to look down and didn't acknowledge anything going on around him.  Meanwhile this disabled man had to climb over clueless guy all the while hanging on to the pole to help ease himself down into the seat - I offered to help while not being sure what more I could offer - but disabled guy said he was okay, and he thanked me.  Clueless guy - still clueless.

Just wanted to share.  Here's a guy who really needed a seat - I"m sure he's used to asking - I wonder how often people just ignore (or don't hear) him.  I wonder how loud he would get if I didn't hear him when I did.

ALso I noticed that I think the people heading to queens from 59th st have gotten nastier and more aggressive this year.

Post by: SplishSplash on March 16, 2009, 11:40:23 PM
Speaking of stories...

I was on a packed 6 train during morning rush hour--like smooshed to the door packed--and once the doors opened at 51st st. I got out and moved to the side of the door to let others off (like a good subway rider should!). Well, there was a man standing there who would NOT move back to actually let me get out of the way of the open door. I had no where to go and kept getting pushed by the flood of people and thus kept getting pushed back into him. So instead of moving out of the way, he--with BOTH hands--SHOVES me (5'1 petite lady) into the flood of people more.

Shocked, I turned around and, in front of many, many people, said (loudly) "Excuse Me!" Of course he yelled back saying it was my fault b/c I was bumping into him. So I said, still loudly,  "You don't push people like that! move back to let the people off and don't you DARE put your hands on me!"

Perhaps I created a bit of a scene, but good b/c now everyone could see this rude man pushing a lady! I mean, pushing anyone like that is inappropriate unless you are playing hockey!

I was all riled up about it all day though. I couldn't believe it.

One thing that annoys me is when some people feel that they must make their way to the door to be the first ones out, as the subway is nearing or pulling into a stop, when the train is crowded.  More than once I've gotten looks because I don't let go of whatever bar I'm holding in order to let them through.  For some reason I don't have great balance on the subway, and I'm not about to let go of the bar only to fall and look like a fool if the subway suddenly jerks to a stop or accelerates quickly.  When the subway stops I let go and move to let people around me.  These people don't seem to care that the mass of people between themselves and the door are likely getting out as well and that the way to the door will be clear.  Seriously, when the hell is the last time you couldn't get off a subway in time?

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: danootz on May 26, 2009, 03:53:16 PM
I hope the following doesn't come out like too harshly but I am bothered by the argument some have offered here about not glaring and why not just ask for the seat.

I've learned in recent times that ignorance is no excuse for a lot of things in life. If I've heard correctly, people can be ticketed for occupying more than one sit with packages etc. So I'd think that information might find it's way to a fair number of riders. At least the adults who ride the train.

This train is a public space. We're all paying for it in some way or another. I'm a native New Yorker and a homegrown Astorian and I'm fully aware of the circumstances that would make someone occupy more space than they need on the train. I understand and accept mental disabilities or other handicaps as a reason. But as stated before, there are a sickening number of people in New York who have an insane amount of self entitlement and expect others to ASK for a seat or worse yet the seat hoggers get offended that another rider asks for some space.

In my head I can only think "Get a clue" and have some courtesy for fellow passengers. I'll have all the space in the world on the train some times and the moment I know we're hitting a stop I further prepare myself for anyone who needs the space. On the N/Ws, the new ones that have a bar in the middle of the long row you can fit three adults on either side of the bar. I know this and I take it into account when I occupy a seat.

Don't expect people to have to ask for some space. Be aware of your surroundings and know that you should share the space REGARDLESS of whether someone speaks about it or not. These people who sit there taking up two seats, with legs spread out or packages while others stand by deserve to be glared at.

I remember seeing to young guys in suits on a ride home from work I assume, Ditmars bound. Crowded train. One of them took up two seats practically lounging, legs akimbo like nothing mattered and no one on the train existed except for his friend. WTF I thought. He was talking loud and acting hard. You mean to say someone has to deal with this guy to get a seat where you'll either be turned away or have to deal with being next to him for however many stops? Ridiculous.
Title: Re: Subway Etiquette

They always do move it once they see I'm in the process of sitting down.
Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: megc on May 26, 2009, 04:49:35 PM
The reason I believe that people don't "just ask", is that a lot of people don't want to deal with a potential public confrontation.  I've seen people flip out on more than one occasion, and sometimes it has ended violently.  Some of us are less assertive when it comes to seats, or even asking someone to turn down the music (I fear for a whole generation of people with tinnitus in their future).

These days, if I see what looks like a space, I politely say "excuse me" and sit down.  It was harder when I was using a cane, for some reason.  My suspicion is that it was because I was already feeling self conscious, and didn't want to bring more attention to myself.  My issue, I know.  

These days, I look up and around much more and give up my seat more often to pregnant women, parents with children, and the disabled (including those with canes).  When I'm alert and paying attention, it feels good to help in this way.

Another fun one today.  This extremely creepy old guy sat a little too close to me (plenty of room was available), put both of his hands on my shoulder, and asked if I had the time.  I said I didn't (true).  Silence for a moment as we stare at each other, his hands still on my shoulder.  Then, "Oh, you don't have the time?"  Me:  "No.  Stop touching me."  He retreated.  I thought it was over, and returned to my book.  Then a few stops later he got off, but not before again placing both hands on my shoulder and saying "I'll see you around, ok?"  Me, leaning away and wearing an incredulous and slightly terrified expression:  "Bye".

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: billyboy on June 04, 2009, 01:58:25 PM
Had a weird rush-hour train ride this morning, getting on at Ditmars.  Trained filled up fast and then sat there for a few minutes.  On my left was a woman breast-feeding her baby, both breasts out, and not only that, she was farting, and talking loudly on a cellphone the entire time with her free hand.  (She would occasionally give me a squirt in the eye with her free breast when she caught me glancing over.)

On my right was Kempster making out passionately with his boyfriend.  How did I know it was him?  He was dressed exactly as his avatar, save for a steamed-up monocle.  (He kept poking my leg with his walking cane, too.)

Across from us was the entire contingent of  belligerent alcoholics whove turned up again on the southeast corner of Ditmars and 31st, carrying on as usual, trying to sing Come Sail Away by Styx and getting each other in headlocks. 

The air smelled of gasoline, burning maple syrup and cow manure.  Some wise acre must have slipped into the conductors booth, because the PA system was broadcasting church bells playing Faith of Our Fathers.

As we pulled out, the Christ Is Coming girl came through the subway door, banging on a tambourine and exhorting everyone to find the one true savior.  I couldnt see her so well because there were three heavily pregnant women carrying bags in each arm standing around me.  When she saw what was going on, her head started spinning like Linda Blairs in The Exorcist, and she appeared to be speaking in tongues.

The train ground to a halt between the Ditmars and Astoria Blvd. stations and sat there for four hours.  I just got into work a few minutes ago: my hair is now white and I cant get my teeth to stop chattering.  A coworker told me Kung Fu just died in Bangkok, and Im thinking Ive seen better days on the train.

Cockroaches then moved into the debris, causing panic among more innocent homeowners.

No longer wanting to eat in their roach-infested kitchens, they researched where to find the best pizza, but riots ensued. Instead, they brought their babies into nice restaurants with them, and more riots ensued when the babies disturbed the child-hating patrons. So they got on line for the Beer Garden, but didn't like dealing with the frat boy crowd, and headed over to Studio Square.

After putting the kids to sleep with beer in their bottles, they returned home to their bedbug-infested beds, falling asleep while reading the Cadillac Man's new book.

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: artgirl on June 29, 2009, 03:53:09 PM
Re: wanting to sit when you see someone taking up more than one seat...

If I want to sit down, and someone has their bag(s) taking up a seat, I always just ask politely with a smile. No one has ever given me an issue about it.
However, I got an unusual response last week...

It was evening rush hour (probably 6:00-ish), and I got on a W at 23rd St. There was one unoccupied seat, which was being taken up by a guy's plastic shopping bag, which he was holding onto. So I went over, gestured to the seat, and said "excuse me, may I sit?". Guy took his headphones off because he didn't hear me, so I politely repeated myself. His response: "I'll let you sit if you tell me where you're from". I was in no mood, so I rolled my eyes, said "ok, buddy", turned and stood across the way until another seat opened up down the car a couple stops later.

Cheesy pickup line, or ploy to hog 2 seats? If it was the latter, it worked  :roll:

Just a quick survey - where is the best place to stand when your friend is using one of the two metrocard machines?

1.  Directly in front of the second machine so that no one can use it and a line forms.
2.  Right next to your friend, but out of the way.
3.  Other

If five people are waiting, and you hear a train coming into the station, and the people in the line ask you if you are in line as you don't appear to be using the machine, and it's unclear if you are waiting to buy metrocards, if the second machine is broken or if you're just blithering idiots, what do you do?

1.  Ignore the people, why should you respond?
2.  Continue your loud conversation and pretend you can't hear.
3.  Step aside and let the people you are making miss the train have a shot at getting to work on time?

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: jennifer on October 10, 2009, 11:25:06 AM
Kids should get to sit in a seat.  That said - a parent knows their child and if a parent is polite and they see someone who seems to need a seat more than their child they should move the child to the lap IF the child will sit there calmly.  If you child won't sit on your lap and the person isn't elderly, disabled, obviously pregnant or doesn't tell you she has some reason she needs to sit - they I think saying "no" is fine. 

as far as the small enough to ride for free slogan.... it's great - but only if you have a kid who will sit on your knee.

Was the no eating or drinking rule discontinued?
Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: lanseaux on June 09, 2010, 08:53:31 PM
The only thing they did was ban having any liquid in an "open container".  (Not that I've ever seen that enforced.)

§ 1050.6, para. 6:  No person shall bring or carry onto a conveyance any liquid in an open container.

I think it was proposed to ban all eating and drinking, but it didn't go through.  Drinking alcohol is prohibited, but it was before as well.

http://www.mta.info/nyct/rules/rules.htm

Title: Re: Subway Etiquette
Post by: RayG on June 10, 2010, 01:08:40 PM
Quote from: AlexNYC on June 09, 2010, 08:31:18 PM
Speaking of subway etiquette, the eating and drinking gets to be too much, especially in the morning. People having egg sandwiches and balancing their coffee between their feet, which of course ends up spilling and making most of the floor sticky. Around 3 years ago I recall reading that the MTA made eating and drinking on the subway against the rules and one could get fined. I never saw any signs or people observing the rules. Was the no eating or drinking rule discontinued?

Yes, it was... Several years ago Mayor Dinkins was riding the train, and several photogs caught him drinking a cup of coffee while on board. It was mentioned to him that eating and drinking were prohibited. He apologized for "sending the wrong message" and shortly thereafter they dumped the rule.
